For this reason a major objective of the Apollo 14 mission, was to\nsample material comprising the Fra Mauro Formation, which had been interpreted as being a portion of the ejecta blanket deposited during the impactformation of the Imbrium Basin (Gilbert, 1893; Eggleton, 1964; Wilhelms,\n1970). This event was believed to predate mare formation, and it was hoped\nthat an age for the Imbrian event could be established through successful\nreturn of these samples.\nt\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe geological formations in the area of the landing site are shown in\nfigure 1. The formations shown on the map are subdivided into four age\ngroups: pre-Imbrian (oldest), Imbrian, Eratosthenian, and Copernican\n(youngest), in accordance with U.S. Geological Survey usage. Imbrian formations predominate the region of the Apollo 14 landing site. This area is\nknown as the Fra Mauro region, and is a light-colored, topographically high\narea surrounded by maria.\nThe Fra Mauro region and the Fra Mauro Formation are named for the crater\nFra Mauro, an ancient, eroded, and partially buried crater 70 km south of\nthe landing site. The Fra Mauro Formation is a distinctive ridged and furrowed unit surrounding the Imbrium Basin. This formation had traditionally\nbeen interpreted as fragmental ejecta from the Imbrium Basin since the idea\nwas first proposed by G. K. Gilbert in 1893. In recent years, however, this\nidea has met with some challenge by investigators who point out that much\nlocal material excavated by secondary cratering is admixed with the Imbrium\nBasin material (~lorrison and Oberbeck, 1975).\nThe thickness of the Fra Mauro Formation is not known, and estimates vary by\nan order of magnitude. Offield (1970) estimates the thickness to be between\n100 and 200 meters based on its relation to t~e local topography, while\nKovach (1971) finds it to be only 20 - 70 meters thick, using results from\nthe active seismic experiment by the Apollo 14 astronauts. It is seen to\ncover 26,000 km 2 in the region of the landing site, feathering to a thin\nedge in the vicinity of the crater Bonpland approximately 150 km to the\nsouth. A northwest-trending ridge, radial to the Imbrium Basin lies about\n600 meters east of the smooth terrain of the landing site. At the crest of\nthis ridge is Cone Crater, a relatively young crater 340 meters deep.\nHere, it becomes important to know the thickness of the Fra Mauro Formation,\nfor some of the material collected at station C1 (figure 2) is interpreted\nto be Cone Crater ejecta. The question is whether this material is from the\nFra Mauro Formation, or from the underlying material. The relief of the\nridge is 90 meters. If Cone Crater is only 70 - 80 meters deep, chances are\nthe ejecta represents Fra Mauro material.\n\n5 - 78-28747\n\nFig ure la. In the case of lunar samples, this model is relatively\nsimple, but the resulting samples can be extremely complex. Primitive lunar\ncrust is assumed to have been coarse-grained ipneous material. Coarsegrained igneous lunar samples have old ages (most have crystallization ages\nolder than 4.0 billion years ago), hypidiomorphic-granular texture with a\ngrain size generally greater than 1 mm, anorthositic compositions or high\nMg/Fe ratios suggestive of a cumulate origin, low rare-earth values and\npositive Europium anomalies, and have low-siderophile element concentrations (Warner et.~., 197.1).\nThis primitive coarse grained material is assumed to be older than most of the\nfine-grained igneous material. This fine-grained material includes mare flood\nbasalts and volcanics with associated pyroclastics. Tilese samples have igneous textures, lack mineral and lithic clasts, have low-siderophile concentrations, and contain relativelY little metallic iron (Warner et ~., 1974).\nThe primary geologic process acting on this primitive material has been\nmeteroid bombardmpnt. The major phase of this bombardment was accomplished\nbefore the emplacement of the flood basalts, but has continued up to present\ntimes. Meteoroid bombardment constitutes the major weathering and rock\nforming agent acting on the lunar surface. Lunar soils and breccias form\nas a result of meteoroid impact, but there has been much debate over the\nactual lithification process. Because of the close chemical and mineralogic\nresemblance LSPET (1969) believed lunar breccias to be shock lithified soil.\nTwo schools of thought emerged, with investigators such as King et al. (1970),\nMason et al. (1970), Quaide and Bunch (1970), Shoemaker et al. (197Qf, Wood\net al.-r1970) favoring shock welding and Smith et al. (1970~ McKay et al.\n(1970), and McKay and Morrison (1971) preferring-thermal welding as the-rithification process responsible for breccia formation. Chao et~. (1971) believed breccias formed by low level shock compaction of the soil near the\nbase of the regolith. He felt that this would occur at some distance from\nthe impact. Studies of Apollo 14 breccias indicated thermal metamorphism\nto be the most reasonable model for breccia lithification (Warner, 1972;\nJackson and Wilshire, 1972; Wilshire and Jackson, 1972; Chao et al., 1972).\nMore recently, using SEM techniques, Simonds et al. (1977) an~Phinney et al.\n(H76) suggested that breccias form when hot silicate melt welds the relatively cool clastic fragments together during meteorite impact.\nThe collection of rocks returned by the Apollo 14 mission consists of breccias, most of which are compound (or polymict) in their nature, and a few\nbasalt samples. This is consistent with the idea that the Apollo 14 landing\nsite was on the Fra Mauro Formation. A breccia is a rock consisting of angular coarse-grained fragments in a fine grained matrix. Commonly, in the\ncase of lunar breccias, there is no definite distinction between "matrix" and\n"clasts" because of the seriate texture of the rock. In these cases, we refer to fragments larger than 1 mm as clasts and those smaller than 1 mm as\nmatrix to be consistent with the practices of the curatorial staff.\n8\n\n,"\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n()\n\n,..\n\n..\n\nBreccias have a higher siderophile element concentration than do lunar rocks\nof igneous origin implying that they contain some admixed meteoritic ~aterial.\nThey contain both mineral and lithic clasts and have more metallic iron than\ndo either the fine-grained or coarse-grained igneous rocks.\nThe return of so many breccias as a result of the Apollo 14 mission made it\nimportant to devise a breccia classification scheme, and many investigators\nhave done so. Wilshire and Jackson (1972) chose a simple descriptive means\nof classification which enables the rocks to be placed in categories (Fl-F4)\nprimarily on the basis of the color index of their clasts and the sample\'s\ncoherence, a useful classification allowing the rocks to be categorized on\nthe basis of hand specimen examination (Table 3).\nTABLE 3\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nBasic Breccia Classification Scheme\nof Wilshire and Jackson (1972)\nLIGHT\nCLASTS\nDARK\nCLASTS\n\n~ ).\n.......\n~,\n\nFRIABLE\n\nCOHERENT\n\nFollowing more extensive observations, it was possible to invent more elaborate schemes, and a debate arose over the classification as well as over the\norigin of these breccias. Much of the debate stemmed from the lack of agreement on the stratipraphic history of the landing site (see General Geology).\nMost researchers had accepted the Fra Mauro Formation as being an ejecta\nblanket associated with the Imbrian event, and, there is undoubtedly a\nsizable contribution of material from the many post-Imbrian cratering events.\nThis contribution could be merely a thin veneer-mixed with Imbrium ejecta or\nit could even be a thick regolith developed on Fra Mauro basalts as was suggested by Schonfeld and Meyer (~973). Many workers now accept the arguments\nof Chao (1972), Morrison and Oberbeck (1975), and Head and Hawke (1975) that\nthe high degree of thermal effects in the rocks is more consistent \\~ith their\norigin by nearby smaller, pre-Imbrian events. This has recently been revie\\~ed by Hal\'lke and Head (1977).\nAlmost as many methods of classifications of these breccias were developed\nas there were articles written about them. Some, such as Chao et al. (1972),\nformed groups on teh basis of the clasts, thereby deriving geneticlre1ationships. Chao\'s classification system is based on fragment population, nature\nof matrix, grain size and porosity, metamorphic history, and bulk chemical\ncomposition. He divided the Apollo 14 breccias into regolith microbreccias,\nFra Mauro breccias, and spherule-rich microbreccias (Table 4).\n\n9\n\nTABLE 4\nBreccia Classification System\nof Chao et tl. (1972)\n1. Regolith microbreccias\na. Unshocked, porous\nb. Compact, nonporous\nc. Shocked\n2. Fra Mauro breccias\na. Unannealed or slightly annealed,\nfeldspathic breccias\nb. Moderately annealed breccias\nc. Strongly annealed (thermally metamorphosed)\nbreccias\nUnshocked\nShocked\n3. Spherule-rich, transported microbreccias\n\nThis classification is roughly comparable to that of Jackson and Wilshire\n(1972). Their Fj is equivalent to the unshocked, porous regolith microbreccia of Chao et al. (1972); F2 resembles the compact or shocked regolith\nmicrobreccia; F3-rs-analogous to unannealed (Fra Mauro) friable feldspathic\nmicrobreccia, and F4 resembles the strongly annealed (Fra Mauro) breccias\nof Chao et~. (1972).\nThe distinction between shocked and unshocked samples in the classification\nof Chao et ~. (1972) is mainly on the basis of the presence or absence\nof microrractures that cause the microbreccias to break across, rather than\naround, grain boundaries. Other shock features include shock-induced\nlamellar twinning in ilmenite grains, low porosity, and glass coating on\nthe microbreccia chips. Compact regolith microbreccias that show no evidence of shock features were also observed (143l3).\nThe gradation between unannealbd to annealed breccias is analogous to that\nof Warner\'s (1972) low to high metamorphic grades. This model included three\nmetamorphic grades, low, medium, and high. He was able to form eight groups\n(1-8) corresponding to these grades. These were formed on the basis of\nabundance of matrix glass, abundance of glass clasts, and matrix texture.\nIt was suggested that with increasing temperature, glass clasts and spherules devitrify and lose identity, while pyroxene and feldspar recrystallize\ndeveloping more euhedral crystals, until, at the highest temperatures, the\nmatrix melts. These 8 groups were correlated with temperature by Williams\n(1972) who found the range from 500 o -1100\xc2\xb0C to be sufficient to produce the\nobserved features. Magnetic properties of the Apollo 14 samples correlate\nwell with the metamorphic classification of Warner (1972). All observed\nmagnetic characteristics can be attributed to the increase in grain size\n\n,.\n\n10\n\n.. -- .. ,\n\n..\n\n_\n\n-~\n\n,.,.\n\n.1. ,\n\no\n\nof interstitial iron from the 100 A range in Warner\'s lowest metamorphic\ngrade samples to grains larger than 1 ~m in the highest grade samples (Gose\net li., 1972)\nQuaide and Wrigley (1972) saw three groups: regolith breccias, white\nrock breccias, and annealed breccias. Others believed the matrix provided\na good classification standard. Von Engelhardt et a1. (1972), using glass\ncontent as a criterion, divided breccias into 3 groups, each matched by a\nproposed origin:\nI. Glass-rich breccias - produced by meteorite impacts on regolith\nII. Glass-poor breccias - produced by impacting solid rock\nIII. Glass-poor breccias with a crystalline matrix - produced by\nY-ecrystallization of a base surge deposit or an impact melt\n\n.\n\nChristie et a1. (1973) emphasize textural features, forming two groups\n(A & B) on-the basis of the presence or absence of evidence of recrystallization, and Lindsay (1972) formed two groups, I and II, on the basis of the\npresence or absence of glass.\nLater, more detailed studies of the matrices of the Apollo 14 breccias using\nthe SEM indicated that the texture of Warner\'s group 1-7 is "heterogeneous\nand intermingled on a scale of millimeters" (Simonds et a1., 1977). Based\non these studies Simonds et a1. (1977) have identifie~3lJreccia groups,\nwhich bear some resemblance to the groups of von Engelhardt et al. (1972).\nThe three groups they propose are:\n-Crystalline matrix breccias (CMB) - those with coherent ho10crysta1line matrices, at least vestiges of clasts, and meteorite contamination\n(evidenced by high siderophile content).\nVitric matrix breccias (VMB) - impactites with a definite fragmental\ntexture and abundant glass, glass-bearing clasts, and low melting point\nclasts.\nLight matrix breccia~ (LMB) - friable, porous, fragmental breccias,\nwith little glass, lacking in recrystallization effects, and more\nfe1dspathic than the other two categories.\nSimonds et a1. (1977) further subdivide their crystalline matrix breccias\ninto 3 subgroups:\n(1) Clast-free impact melts (14310, 14276)\n(2) Clast-bearing impact melts - 1-15% clasts (14068)\n(3) Fra Mauro breccias - more than 15% clasts (all other Apollo 14 CMB\'s)\nIndeed, many of the Apollo 14 breccias are described by them as being crystalline matrix breccias of the Fra Mauro type.\nTable 5 contains a list of Apollo 14 rocks and their classification by various investigators. Lunar\nanorthosites have a granulitic texture.\n\n..\n\nbasalt - a fine grained, usually dark colored, igneous rock \\~hich commonly\nis extrusive in origin. Composition of basalt, ordinarily, includes\nprimarily calcic plagioclase, pyroxene, and other mafic minerals, such\nas olivine.\nbreccia - a clastic rock with angular and broken rock fragments in a finer\ngrained matrix. For purposes of this catalog, breccia fragments larger\nthan 1 mm are designated as clasts, and those smaller than 1 mm are referred to as matrix.\nclast - fragmental part of a breccia larger than 1 mm.\nmin~ra1, or glass in lunar breccias.\n\nA clast may be lithic,\n\ncoherent - consolidated; not friable; doesn\'t crumble easily\ncrystallite - a broad term applied to grains or crystals which are too small\nfor identification\ndendritic - minerals that have crystallized in a branching or feathery\npattern, commonly in a glassy matrix\ndevitrified - ~aid of a glass which has converted to a crystalline texture\nafter its solidification\nfragmental rock - any clastic rock; rock composed of fragments of other rocks,\nminerals, or glass; includes breccias and microbreccias\nfriable - said of a rock that crumbles naturally or is easily broken; poorly\nconsolidated; pocr1y cemented; not coherent\ngabbro - coarse grained equivalent of basalt, commonly intrusive in origin\n"glass" - a term used ill optic~l petrographic descriptions to denote\namphorous and/or cryptocrystalline material\n1eucocratic - light colored; applied to a light-colored igneous rock relatively poor in mafic minerals\n\n17\n\n. o\xc2\xb7\n\n,\n\n..\n\n,,"\n\n.\n\nmatrix - smaller or finer grained material filling interstices between the\nlarger grains or particles of a rock; for purposes of this catalog,\nportion of a bl\'eccia smaller than 1 mm in size\nmelanocratic - dark colored; usually applied to dark colored igneous rock\nrich in mafic minerals\nmelt rock - igneous rock whi ch is nei ther i ntrus i ve nor ex tl\'US i ve in ori gi n,\nbut formed by melting during meteoroid impact. These rocks resemble\nbasalts, but have "ghost clasts," or small areas of textural homogeneity enclosed in a fabric of a different texture; occasionally these exhibit remnant clasts, which appear as xenocrysts\nmesocratic - composed of subequal amounts of light and dark constituents;\napplied to igneous rocks intermediate in color bet\\~een leucocratic\nand melanocratic\nmesostasis - the last-formed interstitial material, either glassy or aphanitic, of an igneous rock\nmicrobreccia - a bl\'eccia \\~ith no clasts larger than 1 mm; a clast of a\nbreccia from previous generation within another breccia\nnorite - a coarse grained plutonic rock containing calcic plagioclase and\northopyroxene\nophitic - texture of a holocrystalline, hypidiomorphic rock in IVhich lathshaped plagioclase crystals are partially or completely enclosed by\npyroxene crystals\nphenocryst - a term used for a large crystal in a porphyritic igneous rock\npoikilitic - igneous rock texture in which small crystals of one mineral\n(e.g. plagioclase) are irregularly scattered without cOll111on orientation\nin a larger crystal of another mineral (e.g. pyroxene)\npolymict - said of a breccia containing fragments of differiny composition\nseriate - texture in which grain size varies gradually or in a continuous\nseri es from 1al\'ge to small\n\n(\n\nsubophitic - said of the ophitic texture of an igneous rock in which the\nfeldspar crystals are approximately the same size as the pyroxene and\nare only partially enclosed by them\ntroctolite - a coarse grained plutonic rock containing calcic plagioclase\nand olivine, with little or no pyroxene\nzap pit - micrometeoroid impact crater on a lunar sample; commonly lined\n\\~ith glass\n18\n\n.~\xc2\xb7i\n\n, .Fl\n\nI\n\n"\'.I\n\nI . \'. 1 \xe2\x80\xa2.\xe2\x80\xa2,,1\' \xe2\x80\xa2. _1-.: c..:.1.c;....!:c:.\n\n.".LLJ:"~...;;.lt?"::J:\':T.,~\xc2\xb7,t\':;:~E . . . ]!,\'~:L..\xe2\x80\xa2 IF":I\' \xc2\xb7\xc2\xb7\xc2\xb7~"I\\,"\'\'\'\':;:~\xc2\xb7\'::::Yc.:{;\xc2\xb7g,:I.Y,~~\n\nAPOLLO 14 ROCK SAMPLE\nDESCRIPTIONS\n\ni\nI\n\nI\nL\n\nI .\n, \xe2\x80\xa2... .\nI ..\n,\nI..\n\n,\n\nI\n,.\n\n\\\n\nI\nI\n\n.,"\n\n19\n\n140 06\n\nORIGINAL PAGE IS\n~\nOF POOR QU.A\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n5 -71-25 297\n\n140 06,5\n\n140 06, 21\n\ni.1 mmi\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nNote, Variati on In\n\n9la~s\n\n20\n\nconten t.\n\nSample 14006 was collected as part of the contingency sample during the first\nEVA in the vicinity of the lunar module. It was returned in weigh bag 1039\nalong with the rest of the contingency sample.\nPHYSICAL CHARACTERISTICS\nDimensions\n\nMass\n12.13\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n<>\n\n3.0 x 2.0 x 1.3 em\n\ng\n\nThis rock is a coherent, dark medium gray breccia with leucocratic clasts comprising less than one percent of the sample. Thin section examination reveals the sample to be inhomogeneous with respect to glass content.\nSURFACE FEATURES\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSample 14006 is pitted with one fresh surface. The pits are glass lined and\nvary in size from 0.3 to 0.7 mm. Pit density is very low.\nVugs are present, covering less than 5% of the surface of the sample. They\nrange in size from 0.1 to 0.5 mm and are spaced approximately 4 mm apart.\nThey are irregular to subcircular in shape, and are homogeneous in distribution. Plagiocl,ase crystals projecting from the walls are clear to gray,\nbut were not observed in thin section. One non-planar fracture cuts the\nrock. The fracture surface texture appears typical of that of the rest of\nthe sample.\nPETROGRAPHIC DESCRIPTION\nIn thin section 14006 is a fine grained, texturally heterogeneous, fragmental\nrock .. Less than one percent of the rock is composed of 1 mm clasts. These\nare composed of plagioclase cumulates with minor olivine (40%), pale yellow\npyroxene (10%), gray pyroxene (50%), and scattered opaque minerals. The\nmatrix is very fine grained with some glass. There are small masses of a\nreddish-orange glass present as distinct units in the matrix. The matrix\nis nearly opaque and shows gradation of size from very fine-grained up to\nrather coarse-grained. The grains that comprise the matrix material appear\nto be si ngl e crystal fragments to small 1ithi c fragments.\nVugs are round to irregular and comprise approximately 5% of the sections.\nThe size of the V\xc2\xb7Jgs vary from 0.1 mm up to 0.5 mm. There are no crystals\nprojecting into any of the vugs.\nThe clasts (> 1 mm fragments) consist of a wide variety of lithic fragments\nas well as single mineral grains. Among the lithic clasts present are the\nfollowing: plagioclase cumulates with olivine; fine-grained polymict breccias; and a few devitrified glass fragments. The remainder of the clasts\nconsist of single crystal fragments of plagioclase and pyroxene. Many of\nthe pyroxene grains show reaction rims. Most of the mineral grains show\nwell developed cleavage patterns. Many of the larger plagioclase grains\nare mUlti-twinned.\n21\n\nThe fragments throughout the sections show little to no shock effects. Some\nof the plagioclase forms radiating bundles which may be a devitrification\nfeature. Several "ghost" clasts are present which are clasts that blend in\nwith the matrix in such a manner as to make their distinction very difficult.\nThese clasts, however, \'are distinct in texture and composition as to distinguish them one from another. Localized pods of fine grained matrix occurs\nintermingled with stringers of coarser grained matrix surrounding it.\n\n{~!\n\nU\n\nDISCUSSION\nSample 14006 is one of the rocks studied by Warner (1972), Wilshire and\nJackson (1972), Chao et al. (1972), Quaide and Wrigley (1972), von Engelhardt\net al. (1972), and SimondS et al. (1977). They agree that it is a glass poor\nbreccia with a crystalline matrix. Chao et al. (1972) classify it as unshocked strongly annealed Fra Mauro breccia (2CT.--Warner (1972) and Chao et al.\n(1977) list it as having been thermally metamorphosed. Simonds et al--.(1977)\nclassiFy it as a CMS (see Table 4, p.\n). Simonds et \xc2\xa31. (1977)studied\nsample 14006,5. Our group examined 14006,7 and 14006,21 and found the matrix\nto consist of 5 - 6% glass. These sections appear to be more like the LMS\'s\nof Simonds et al., or the glass-poor with a fragmental matrix rocks of\nvon Engelhardtet al. (1972). \';hey do not have enough glass to be classed as\na VMS, but neither-Sample 14006,7 nor sample 14006,21 is a CMS. On the\nother hand, the thin sections show the clear cut difference from one thin\nsection to another -- 14006,5 appears to have a crystalline matrix with little\nor no glass visible.\nPhinney et al. (1976) studied sample 14006,6 and described it as a tough,\ncrystallTnelJreccia with 15 - 20% vugs and vesicles. The matrix is described as consisting of 10 - 20 ~m plagioclase and clinopyroxene, with 5 ~m\nilmenite and smaller than 5 ~m interstitial potassium feldspar. Grains are\nmostly equigranular and interlocking, but some are subhedral in shape.\nSample 14006 is said to have the typical major and trace element chemistry\nof Apollo 14 basaltic breccias of KREEP composition (Hubbard et al., 1972)\nexcept that both have low K and Rb concentrations accompaniedlby-rncreases\nin K/Rb ratios (Gibson and Hubbard, 1972).\nScanning electron microscope stUdies (McKay et ~., 1972) reveal that 14006\nhas vapor phase deposited minerals lining cavities. A limit at 1050\xc2\xb0C has\nbeen placed on the temperature to which this sample was heated, because the\nsample has been depleted in K and Rb but not Na, and because of the vapor\nphase deposition (Gibson and Hubbard, 1972).\n\n22\n\nc \'__\n\n. __ ,,\' .\',\n\n.,;"".,-- :\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n..\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n14007\n\n,0\n\n1cm\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n$-71-25324\n\nSample 14007 was collected as part of the contingency sample during the first\nEVA in the vicinity of the lunar module. It has a homogeneous texture with\nan average grain size of less than 0.1 mm. One of the 1eucocratic lithic\nclasts on the N face is 14 mm in size (S-76-23341).\nThin section 14047,53 shows two main types of clasts. The first is a crystalline breccia with large pyroxene and plagioclase shards. The second\ntype is large shards of partly devitrified to fresh yellowish glass. Where\ndevitrification has taken place the crystals are dendritic and scattered\nalong fractures and near the boundary of the glass. One large mass of\nglass shows residual pyroxene granules showing a skeletal pattern isolated\nin the glass. In hand specimen there appears to be no glass or mineral\nfragments present, however, in thin section they are abundant. In the\nthin section, there is approximately 30% glass in the matrix. The glass\nis a "dirty" yellow brown and discontinuous. Several spheres of yellow\nglass are also present. The unusual feature of the section is the large\namount of fresh glass.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMegascopica11y, 1% of the fragments are greater than 1 mm. Leucocratic\nlithic fragments compose 20% of the rock. These white fragments have\n.\nolive brown spots. (Not so abundant in thin section.) T-he opaque content\nis extremely low, consisting of angular to subrounded clasts of ilmenite\nwith subordinate metallic iron in a fine grained silicate matrix. In order\nof decreasing abundance, ilmenite, metal, troilite and a few small (10 \\lm)\ngrains of chromian spinel are present.\nDISCUSSION\nSample 14047 was described as friable with leucocratic clasts and placed\nin their Fl category by Wilshire and Jackson (1972). It was described as\nbeing low grade metamorphic and placed in group 1 of Warner (1972). Chao\net ~. (1972) described it as an un shocked porous regolith microbreccia,\nplacing it in his la category. Simonds et a1. (1977) classify it as a\nVMB. It was\nplaced in bag 6N by the astronauts who said they collected it from a crater\nrim. The lunar location and orientation are not documented by lunar surface\nphotographs.\nPHYSICAL CHARACTERISTICS\nMass\n\nDimensions\n8.0 x 5.0 x 4.0 cm\n\n200.13 g\n\nThis fragmental rock has less than 1% subrounded leucocratic clasts in a\nmedium gray matrix and is extremely friable and soft.\nSURFACE FEATURES\nSample 14049 is lacking in zap pits and has no cavities or fractures\nvisible on the surface.\nPETROGRAPHIC DESCRIPTION\xc2\xb7\nThe rock is very fine grai,led and appears homogeneous.\nwere identified include transparent feldspar, greenish\nand one dark brown spherul e. No fragments al\'e greater\nare no opaques in the matrix except for several ragged\n100 ~m in diameter.\n\nMineral grains that\nand brownish glass,\nthan 1 mm. There\nmetal grains up to\n\nThin section 14049,0 is somewhat similar to 14047,53 in that there is a\nlarge amount of fresh glass shards in the matrix. There is approximately\n20% yellow glass in the bonding material that holds the clasts and fragments.\nThere is one large elongate glass glob with several rounded and eroded pyroxene crystals in it. There are no lithic or mineral clasts present. There is\nat least two different matrix fabrics present with a sharp dividing line between them. Unlike 14047,53, some of the glass has undergone some devitrification. The only lithics present are plagioclase-rich microbreccias and\npyroxene-rich microbreccias. The mineral fragments are about equal amounts\nof pyroxene and plagioclase.\nDISCUSSION\nThis is one of the softest rocks of the Apollo 14 mission. A shocked basaltic fragment consisting of diaplectic plagioclase glass and pyroxene with\nlamellae were found in sample 14049 and reported by von Engelhardt et al.\n(1972), who classify the sample as a glass rich regolith breccia. The convex surface has\ngentle relief with some prominences of approximately 1-3 mm in height.\nThese features are not abrupt but rounded. Pits appear on the convex\nsurface but not on the flat surface. Pits are glass lined and have a size\nrange from 0.5-3.0 mm. Average pit size is 1 to 2 mm. Density of pits\nis approximately 10 pits per square centimeter.\nCavities are both vesicular and vuggy. Irregular vugs range from 1-2 mm\nln size. Small vugs {0.5 mm diameter} and vesicules {1 mm in diameter}\ncontain feldspar and orthopyroxene crystals.\n\nI\xc2\xb7\xc2\xb7\xc2\xb7..\n\n~.J\n\ni\n\nI: !\n\'I~\xc2\xb7I\n\'.\' I\n, I\n\n.\n\n1\'\n\n;\',\n\ni\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n:, j\n-!\n\n""""-\n\nThere are three planar and four non-planar fractures appearing in two sets.\nFrdctures have an irregular orientation. Approximately 30% of section 14053,6 consists of plagioclase laths, and the remalnder is pyroxene. There are, as is seen in hand specimen, two types of\npyroxene present. The higher value assigned to feldspar {60%} in hand\nspecimen may be due to the inhomogeneous nature of the rock. In texture\nand mineral proportion this rock appears different from Apollo 11 and 12\nbasaltic rocks. There is abundant cristobalite {5-10%} and a small amount\nof olivine present in the thin section.\nUpon close examination the rock appears to be inhomogeneous in hand\nspecimen. Some areas display relative concentration of oliv;~e and pyroxene.\nThese inhomogenities are on a 1-2 cm scale.\nDISCUSSION\nSample 14053 is listed as a basalt by Wilshire and Jackson {1972} and by\nQuaide and Wrigley (1972) and as a mare basalt by Simonds et a1. (1977).\nMany, including Swann et al. {1977}, believe that 14053 isiaClast from a\nlarger breccia boulder--. Crystalline rocks are rare in the Apollo 14 collection. In a 1971 memo D. Morrison notes that fragments of soil clinging to\nthe bottom may actually be the remanents of breccia matrix which once enclosed the rock.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSample 14053 is described by Hubbard et al. {1972} as a mare-like basalt,\nwith FeO > 14%, but the Al z03 concentration is more like that of KREEP\nbasalts (> 12%), making 14053 intermediate in composition. Sample 14053\nalso has low REE values, but an intermediate K/U ratio. Ridley (1975)\nconcluded that the sources of aluminous mare basalts such as 14053, are\n52\n\nI,t.\n\'\n\n- - - - - - - - - - - - - - -_________ j~~i;~\n\nnot significantly different from the sources of the common mare basalts.\nHe felt that slight chemical changes in the lunar mantle was more important\nin producing aluminous mare basalts than pressure.\nPa~anastassiou et al. (1971) determined the Rb-Sr age to be 3.96 ~ 0.04 x\n\n10 years for sample 14053. The 38Ar /37Ar th~rmal release patterns allowed\nHusain et~. (1972) to assign an expo~ure age of 21 ~ 5 x 10 6 years to sample\n14053,34, .while the age of formation is given by them as 3.92 \xc2\xb1 0.08 x 10 9\nyears based on 4oAr/ 39 Ar plateau. Age data also suggest that formation of\naluminous mare basalts was not a unique event, but occurred over a 0.5 b.y.\nperiod. During this time the zone of incipient melting moved to progressively higher pressures, but the composition of aluminous mare basalts occupies\na relatively narrow range, supporting Ridley\'s (1975) claim that the chemical changes in the heterogeneous mantle is the significant factor in\naluminous basalt production.\n\nL\'\n\\\n\ni\n\nI\nI\'\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n:1\n~\'1\n\nI\n\nWellk et al. They also identi iied a mare basalt component as well as\nother rock types similar to those previously recognized. They believe that\nthe Ni-rich metal possibly formed in equilibrium with olivine early in\nlunar history. They believe 14063 to be a possible sample of pre-Imbrium\nregolith bases on the absence of Ti-rich spinel and troilite, the rarity of\nmetal, and the lack of metamorphism sufficient to have devitrified glass\nor recrystallized dark-matrix clasts.\n\n~.~ t~\n).)-\n\n~/ .:", -l::j\xc2\xb7l\n\ne:,\n.,..\n\nif\n\n:~\n\n:>~\'l\n\n~. ~\n\nl\n~\n\nj\n\n, \xc2\xb7.\xc2\xb7.iii\n.\n\n\';\':\'j\n1i"\n\n~\n\n"1\n\n\'~h\n\nf\xc2\xb7\xc2\xb7,1\n\'i.\n\n!l\n\nL? 1::\n\nr\n\n69\n\n"\n\n~:. :\n\n~Hi\n\nW\xc2\xb7 .:~:~:a -:~\'~~ "1"1j\n\n!tt. ::ti\n\n.. 1",..\xe2\x80\xa2 ,;.:,1 .. j\n\n"/... ,.. ,...i.tf;l\n\nORIGINA\nOF POO/ PAGE IS\n\n14064\n\nQUALITY\n\n,0\n\nI .:\nj\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\'1cm\nS-71-32451\n\n14064, 34\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n4\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n70\n\n... :!\n\nSample 14064 was collected in the White Rocks are" at Station Cl and was\nreturned in bag 16 along with 14063 and 14065 (residue) in ALSRC 1006.\nThere was a strong feeling at the LRL that 14064 and 14063 are parts of the\nsame rock because not only do they look very much alike, but 14064 has a\nprotruding 1 cm clast, while 14063 has a 1 cm clast mold. She accounts for the observed featu\'res with a petrogeroetic outline of the history of rock 14068:\n(1)\n\nThe melting, involving temperatures beyond the igneous range,\nwas the result of meteorite impact. The molten bleb produced\nwas fairly small.\n(2) The melt picked up plagioclase clasts and nickel-iron blebs.\nThere was sufficient turbulence for the clasts to be mixed\nthroughout the bleb.\n(3) The melt picked up the olivine, orthopyroxene, lithic and\ngl assy fragments. Most of the gl assy cl asts softened and\nvesiculated.\n(4) The melt made contact with some of the dark breccia ("north"\nside) while still quite fluid.\n(5) The melt made contact with the dark breccia on the "south" side\nwhile still plastic, but it was not as fluid as in step 4.\n(6) Rock 14068 has not been involved in any major brecciation or\nthermal metamorphic events since solidification of the olivinerich groundmass was completed.\n\n80\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nt .\nI\n\nI\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 I\n\nj\n1-\'\n\n.1\n\n14069\n\n".\n\n.. .\n~.\n\n,....\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\' .\n.. .\n.~\n\nf\xc2\xb7\n\n.(:\n..\n\n.~\n\n:.\n\nI\n\n..\n\n.,\n\n.\';-\n\n~_!tI.!\'~_\n\nI\n\n1\n\nI\nI\nI\n\n5 -71 - 29157\n\nI\n\n...\n\n~\n\nCD\n\n14069, 4\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI\n81\n\nI\nI\'\nI\n\nSample 14069 was collected at station C\', 1.28 km ENE of the LM and 100 m SE\nof the rim of Cone Crater during the second EVA. Three types\nof vugs were observed: elongate vugs (1.5 mm x 0.25 mm), spherical vugs\n(0.7 mm diameter), and an open network (0.5 mm). The sample appears similar to 14070.\nIn thin section, 14069 is seen to have clasts of pyroxene and plagioclase.\nSome olivine/pyroxene, plagioclase granulitic rock fragments are present in\nthin section 14069,4. Sections 14069,4 and 14069,5 both contain small devitrified fragments. Troi1ite is present in large masses. The samples are composed of 60% plagioclase, 39% pyroxene and 1% olivine. Several pyroxene\ncrystals have reaction rims. The samples are all po1ymict breccias with fragmental\nmatrices.\nSURFACE FEATURES\nGlass lined zap pits are present on samples 14169, 14171,14172,14173,\n14176, 14182, 14185, and samples 14170 and 14187 contain only unlined pits.\nSample 14169 has pits ranging from 0.1 to 2.0 mm in size with a density\nof 10 to 12 pits per square centimeter. One hack1y surface is sparsely\npitted, perhaps indicative of the sample\'s bottom. The rest are less\ndensely pitted. None of the samples contains surface glass.\nThe only cavities present are clast molds on all samples except for 14181\nand 14186. Sample 14181 contains irregularly shaped vugs 0.5 to 1.5 mm in\nsize with a homogeneous disltribution in the matrix only. These are 10 mm\napart and account for less than 1% of the sample\'s volume. Sample 14186\nalso contains irregularly shaped vugs with an average size of 0.8 mm. These\noccur in clusters on one side of the rock. A few others are widely spaced.\n\n130\n\n.\xe2\x80\xa2\n.\n..\n\n\'. !\n\n(1\\\n\\j)\n\nSeveral are lenticular.\n\nNo projecting minerals have been observed.\n\nFractures are present in all samples except 14175 and 14188. The following descriptions are included for\npurposes of completeness:\n\n@)\n\'V,\n\n..\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n14169,8\nThere is a small amount of dark brown "glass" (5-l0%) present in the matrix\nof sample 14169,8. Only one clast larger than 1 mm is present in the section.\nIt is a fine-grained breccia with crystallites scattered throughout. Many of\nthese crystallites appear to be pyroxene. The clast has fractured and the\nmatrix has been injected between the two pieces. A seriate mixture of\nshocked mineral. fragments (including reddish spinel) and three dark clastlike areas are present in the matrix. These "ghost clasts" are areas of\nfine-grained. turbid material with aburdant small crystallites present.\nThese areas merge into the matrix .\n14170,4 &14170,5\nSome "glass" is present in the matrix of breccia 14170. The samples consist\nof a partly crystalline-partly "glassy" matrix with abundant mineral and\nlithic clasts. Several large clasts of pyroxene and basaltic igneous rock\nfragments are scattered throughout the sections. There are fewer clasts in\n14170,4 than there are in 14170,5. Sample 14170,4 contains several devitrified clasts and larger "glassy" breccia clasts. Few, widely scattered,\npyroxene/basaltic clasts are present in section 14170,4 but are common in\n14170,5. Pyroxene crystals are nearly free of inclusions. Basaltic lithic\nclasts have a subophitic texture with anhedral masses of brownish pyroxene\n\n133\n\nand acicular plagioclase. Only small, scattered clasts of plagic,clase are\npresent in either section.\n14171,11\nSection 14171,11 is nearly holocrystalline and contains numerous lithic\nclasts. Lithic clasts include a variety of dark matrix microbreccias, a\nclast of devitrified glass containing abundant dendritic crystals, and a\nsmall, crystalline rock fragment. Opaque minerals are scattered throughout\nthe section. The matrix is a seriate mixture of mineral fragments and\nsmall lithic fragments. Mineral fragments are pyroxene and plagioclase in\napproximately equal proportions.\n14172,7\nSection 14172,7 is a breccia with a "glassy" matrix (30%) in one area,\nwhich grades into a much more crystalline area in other areas (5-10% "glass").\nVery few lithic fragments are present, and only one clast is larger than\n1 mm. This clast consists of a shocked plagioclase crystal with minor\npyroxene adhering to one edge. The crystal is highly fractured and shows\npoorly defined twin planes. Of the identifiable mineral fragments in the\nmatrix, 80% are pyroxene and 20% are plagioclase. Lithic fragments are a\nfine-grained breccia with corroded pyroxene crystals. These crystals contain some inclusions.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n14173,6 & 14173,7\nSection 14173,6 grades from vitric to more crystalline as does 14172,7.\nSample 14173,7, cut from the same rock chip is almost entirely crystalline\nwith a few small masses of a more "gl assy" brecci a. Most cl asts cons i s t\nof pyroxene and plagioclase grains and almost all grains are fractured.\nTwinning is present in ~ome of the pyroxene. A crystalline cumulate is\npresent near the edge of the section. A few small, glassy breccia clasts\nare present in 14173,7. Large fragments present include a few shards of pyroxene, plagioclase, and partly devitrified\nglass. The thin section has a very turbid appearance.\nDISCUSSION\nThe only samples that have received detailed study in this group are 14169\nand 14180. Eldridge et al. (1972) determined potassium, thornum, uranium,\n26A1, and 22Na concentration in these samples using gamma-ray spectrometry.\nThey found a remarkable uniformity in the primordial radio element content\nin all samples. They estimate a KREEP content of 60-85% in Apollo 14 soils\nand breccias on the basis of a two-component mixing model.\n\n135\n\n141 94\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n:\n"\n\n"\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n. :~\n\n, \',,- J .:... :\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n1cm ----,\n\n5-71 - 26965\n\nSamples\nWeigh bag 1031 was used on the trave rse during the second EVA, bag 1031 after\nents that were left in weigh\n14194 -14201 are small rock fragm\ntation\nlocati\nsample s 14306 - 14311 were removed. POOR QUALI1Y\n\nThese samples are pieces of a football-size rock collected from a location\n80 m NNW of the LM during EVA 1. This rock was almost completely buried in\nthe regolith and its orientation is unknown. Both of these samples have\nfreshly broken surfaces. They were first identified as pieces of the same\nrock in March, 1977, when the models of the rock were found to fit together\nalong their freshly broken surfaces. They share a common lithology which is\nespeci"lly evident along the broken surfaces. These samples were returned together in weigh bag 1027 which also contained sample 14305/302 and various\nsmaller chips (see Phinney et al., 1975). These samples were examined in the\nCRA and NNPL by the PET, an~14303 was extensively distributed for scientific\nexperimentation. Sample 14304 has been designated a\' posterity sample.\nPHYSICAL CHARACTERISTICS\nMass\n\nDimensions\n\n14303 898. g\n\n16 x 9 x 7 cm\nI\n\n14304 2499. g\n\n20 x 11 x 10 cm\n\nThis rock is a coherent, gray, blocky to subrounded breccia.\nSURFACE FEATURES\nThe sample is saturated with zap pits and is considerably fractured in some\nplaces. The zap pits range in size from less than 0.1 - 7 mm on 14303 to\n0.1 - 3 cm for 14303. The pits are lined with a dark brown botryoidal to\nbubbly glass. This glass is 0.2 mm thick in the larger zap pits.\nThere are at least two sets of fractures on these samples, so that although\nthe rock is tough with clasts firmly imbedded in the matrix, the rock would\nbreak apart if handled roughly.\nPETROGRAPHIC DESCRIPTION\n14303: This rock is a polymict breccia with a holocrystalline matrix. :he\naverage grain size is approximately 0.2 mm with 95% of the grains less than\n1 mm. Groundmass pyroxenes are\nmainly pigeonites and augites, and are interpreted as being fragments of\nquickly cooled surface basalts.\nRoedder and Weiblen (1972) studied melt inclusions in 14303 and found much\nevidence of a complex thermal history. They note that the groundmass of sample 14303 must have excess (normative) 5i0 2 in it, because all olivine grains\nin contact with it (other than fayalite) have reacted to form thin, almost\nmonomineralic, rims of pyroxene. Both were broken apart during transit to the LRL.\nThe pieces were numbered 14305/14302, and 14303/14304. Sample 14305/302 was\nreturned in weigh bag 1027 along with 14303/304 and fragments 14165-14189.\nAll of these samples have similar lithologies, numerous fractures, and\nfreshly broken surfaces, so the fragments 14165-14189 could be from either\nof the two. It is believed that these football-size rocks may have been\ntransferred into weigh bag 1027 inside the LM, but this transfer was not\ndocumented. They were taken out of 1027 in the CRA before entering NNPL\nfor examination. Sample 14302 was later renumbered 14305,18.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPHYSICAL CHARACTERISTICS\nMass\n14305\n14302 (14305,18)\nCombined\n\nDimensions\n\n2117 g\n380 g\n2497 g\n\n14 x 15 x 10 cm\n14 x 3.5 x 6 cm\n14 x 20 x 10 cm\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe sample is a medium gray, holocrystalline, clast rich breccia which has\nbeen well-sintered together.\n\n,.\n\n:oyj\n.\n\nSURFACE FEATURES\nThe NI face is subrounded and heavily zap-pitted. It contains one large\nglass-lined pit 8 mm in diameter. This is the surface facing up in lunar\nphotographs. The TI , WI, BI , and part of SI (facing E) is made up of a\nflat planar fracture, surface containing no zap pits. The combined rock Ivas\nfound on the regolith surface about two-thirds exposed. The orientation is\nwell-documented by photography, howc~ver, the di stri buti on of zap pits on the\nburied surface indicates a different orientation at an earlier time. The\nplan~r fracture and zap-pit distribution indicate that 14305/302 was exposed\nas part of yet another rock and was subsequently broken off and projected\nto its documented orientation [more detailed discussion can be found in\nSwann et~. (1971) and Horz et~. (1972)].\nExcept for three fractures parallel to the SI planar surface on the SI-BI\nedge, the rock is relatively tough. It is easy to shape with a saw, and\nthe clasts are well sealed in matrix.\nThe turbid and shattered feldspars are the only shock features visible on\nthe surface.\n\nf\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPETROGRAPHIC DESCRIPTION\n14305 is a clast-rich, holocrystalline breccia that has been well-sintered\ntogether. It is composed of a light gray fragmental matrix (~ 70%) with an\naverage grain size less than 0.1 mm (Simonds et al., 1977). The mineralogy\nof the matrix is indeterminate in binocular observation, but appears to be\nplagioclase rich. Only a few percent of mafic silicates are recognizable.\n244\n\nC)\n\nr~~\n\nThe rock has a seriate texture with clasts ranging in size from 10 em down\nto the crystallites comprising the. matrix. Approximately 30% of the rock\nis composed of clasts larger than 1 mm. Most of these clasts (80%) are\nthemselves microbreccias of an older generation. A dominant clast lithology\nis a dark gray aphanitic microbreccia. Only a small percentage of the\nclasts are of a non-fragmental nature. These include white plagioclase-rich\nclasts (15%) and brown mare basalt clasts (5%). All of the clasts are we11cemented to the matrix and cannot be broken free. One dark clast has a black\nglassy matrix contuining 15% phenocrysts and microphenocrysts of subhedra1\nwhite feldspar and pale green olivine. These phenocrysts are approximately\n0.2 mm long set in the crypto-crysta11ine matrix. Maps of 14305,27; 14305,30;\nand 14305,18 (Twede11 et a1., 1978) show the three major clast types (plagioclase, dark gray microbreccia, and mare basalt) as well as several other\nclasts that are interesting.\n\n,\n)\n\n1\n\n1.\n\nI\nj\n\nt\n\nI\'\n\n1\nr\n\n;\nf\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n!1\ni\n\n.1\nI\n\nI\n\nAs can be seen, the major clasts present include a variety of crystalline\nbreccias and/or cumulates and scattered basaltic fragments. Most of the\nsingle mineral clasts lie below the size limitation set and only occasional\nsmall clasts of pyroxene and plagioclase are present. A few clasts of\nanorthositic breccia are also present. Only part of the clasts (as desi]nated) show extensive shock effects. A total of six other sections with\nvarious parents were examined to give a more complete characterization of\nthe rock.\n\nI\n\n\\\n\n,\n\n!\n\n\\\n\nI\n\n1\n;I\n\nThe samples examined with the proper parent designated were:\n\nI\n\nSample\n\nI\n\n14305,4\n\n,\n\nJ\'\nj\n\nIn an attempt to characterize the nature of the sample, a reconstruction\nof slab 14305,46 was undertaken. 14305,46 was subdivided into five portions\nand each of these were made into probe mounts (14305,104-14305,108).\nMicro-photography at approximately 3.75x was undertaken and a photo mosaic\nreconstruction of the slab was made. (1972). It is\nlisted as a grade 6 (high-grade metamorphic) by Warner-r1972) and as a\ncrystalline matrix breccia of the Fra Mauro type by Simonds et al. In their\nstudies of the mineral and chemical variations in these samples-,-Brown et al.\n(1972) found fragments and lenses of rhyolite, which they believed to have-been derived from melting of crystalline granophyre fragments. Several\ngranophyre fragments were observed. K-fe1dspar in one granophyre (14305,111)\ncontains 4.1% BaD. Dence and Plant (1972) note that in section 14305,5\nannealed glass, predominantly of Fra Mauro basalt composition, encloses\nsmall amounts of potassic granite.\nIn their discussion of the effects of micro cratering on the lunar surface,\nGault et a1. (1972) pointed out the deep fracture cutting across the\nspa11ea-region surrounding a large glass-lined pit. This impact apparently\nalmost ruptured the rock, and is a good example of the violent and catastrophic disruption that is caused by particles which are capable of forming a\ncrater with a diameter that is a significant fraction of the rock\'s dimensions discussed in the study by Gault et a1. (1972). Sample 14305 is\ndescribed by Morrison et~. (1972) aSlDeTng heavily cratered on all major\nsurfaces indicating a complex exposure history. A discussion of the surface\norientaticn is presented in an article by Harz et~. (1972).\n\nA photograph of a model of sample 14305/302 is available on page 355 of this\nbooklet, along with its geheo1ogy on page 354.\n\n246\n\n\'.\xc2\xb7-\xc2\xb7\nu)\nI"\n\n"-"\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nJ\n\n14306\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n,\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n5 -77- 22103\n\n247\n\nJ\n\nI\n14306 , 65\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nrs ESE of\nat st at io n G, 230 mete\nental rock co lle ct ed e rim cr es t of North Tr ip le t\nfra\nSample 14306 is a (LMgmand 50 meters E of th\nthan\n),\nmodule\nla r and le ss ir re gu la r\nthe lunar\nbe somewhat more ta bu albedo. This rock is one of those used by Morrison et a1. (1972)\nto present and interpret crater population data in terms of the-r1ux and\nmass distributions of meteoroids in near lunar space. The results of their\nmicrocrater studies were then applied to reconstruct the history of the rocks\nduring their residence time on the lunar surface.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n..\n\nPETROGRAPHIC DESCRIPTION\nThe rock is a coherent, rounded, fragmental rock with some norite clasts and\nfew mare basalt clasts as is evident in the mapped surfaces of 14306,103\n(Twede 11 et a1., 1978).\nThis generic is unlike most of the ocher Apollo 14 rocks in that there is\nan extremely high clast content with only minor "matrix" material. The\nsizes of the clasts vary considerably, but few (in the sections examined)\nexceeded 1 mm in size. The variety of compositions in these clasts is also\nless diverse than in the other generics.\n.\nA total of two thin sections each with a different parent were examined.\nDominant Clast> 1 mm\n\nSample\n\nParent\n\n14306,65\n\n29\n\nGlassy dark metac1astic rock, light metaclastic rock and anorthositic rock.\n\n14306,68\n\n41\n\nFine-grained dark metac1astic rock, 1ightcolored metac1astic rock and anorthositic rock.\n\nThis ana1ysi: is in very good agreement with the work of Wilshire and Jackson (1972). Their work concluded that the dark metac1astic rock was\nrepresented in the largest clasts in the greatest abundance.\nDISCUSSION\nThe sample is classified as a crystalline matrix breccia by Simonds et a1.\n(1977), and like the other CMB\'s it falls into the F4 category of WiTShTre\nand Jackson (1972), grade 4 of Warner (1972), and 2c Fru Mauro (shocked)\nof Chao et iL. (1972). The thermal and mechanical history of sample 14306\nhas been investigated by Anderson et a1. (1972), who describe it as a mu1tigeneration metamorphosed breccia. -rhey found the oldest fragments to be\nbasaltic (noritic) microbreccias that are coarsely crystalline and partly\nglassy. The second generation fragments are dark-gray micro-to-cryptocrystalline po1ymict metabreccias. The third generation is the host matrix,\nand is similar in composition to the second generation.\n\n249\n\nAnderson et al. (1972) interpret the first generation as possibly preimpact in-ag~ It was fragmented and combined with igneous material to\nproduce the second generation, and the third generation \\1aS produced when\nan impact embedded the second generation material in a crystal rich matrix\nand was thrown three crater-diameters from Cone Crater. An interesting\nfeature of this rock is that the glass filling the fracture (SURFACE FEA- m\nTURES) contains three cylindrical metal particles 1-2 mm long and a 200 v\ndiameter metal spherule observed by Morrison et al. (1972). They noticed\nthat one of the metal particles intersects a grass-walled vesicle and that\nsection of the particle is concave conforming to the vesicle wall. The\ntwo other fragments appear to have pulled apart. Although they are now\nseparated by several millimeters, the ends clearly could fit together\n(Morrison et al., 1972). Clasts include four 1 x 2 mm\nvitreous black grains, one 1 x 1 mm light yellow-green olivine, and one\n3 x 3 mm cloudy white plagioclase grain.\nThin section 14309,4 reveals the rock to be a vesicular glassy matrix breccia\nwith approximately 10% "glass" in the matrix. It is fine grained and homogeneous in mineralogy .\nSURFACE FEATURES\nThis rock has a smooth surface and is densely pitted on the two subrounded faces. The other three faces are angular and show no evidence\nof previous microcrater bombardment. The zap pits range in size from\n0.1 mm to 4.0 mm, with approximately 90% of them glass-lined. There are\nestimated to be 20-50 pits per square centimeter on the pitted faces.\nThere are two exfoliation fractures, one of which is para1lei to the fresh\nsurface. There are feldspar-lined vugs 0.5 - 2 mm in diameter covering\n~ 1% of the surface of 14310.\nIn large cavities, the feldspar appears to\nhave a honeycomb texture, but in smaller cavities there are clear to gray\nfeldspar cryst.als projecting into the vugs.\nPETROGRAPHIC DESCRIPTION\nThe rock is composed of feldspar and pyroxene in approximately equal quantities. The feldspar occurs as both clear and turbid euhedral grains of\n0.3 mm size. Pyroxene occurs as turbid subhedra1 grains 0.2 - 0.3 mm in\nsize. Traces of opaques with grains < 0.1 mm are also present. The ro~k is\nmedium grained and very homogeneous in texture.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n<\n\n,.\n\nIn thin section (14310,5), the rock is seen to be composed of 31% pyroxene\n(mostly pigeonite), 68% plagioclase (~ Anso), 0.5% opaques (ilmenite,\nchromite, ulvospinel, and troilite), and a trace of metal, with only 0.5%\nmesostasis. Reid and Melson (LSPET 1971) describe thin sections 14310,4;\n14310,5; 14310,6; as having intersertal to subophitic texture. Platy plagioclase forms a framework, with individual crystals as long as 1 mm, and\nlength/width of approximately 30. Pigeonite, possibly rimmed in places by\naugite, occurs in interstitial to subophitic to sheathlike grains. Discrete\nareas (as large as 500 llm across) consist of a felted aggregate of plagioclase (and minor alkali feldspar?) laths with a grain size noticeably smaller\nthan the rest of the section. What can be described as a "cognate xenolith"\nconsists of an area 3.3 x 1.5 mm, with the same phases as the remainder of\nthe section, but with a noticeably finer grain size. It is this sort of\narea that suggests that basalt samples such as 14276 and 14310, for example,\nwere melted during impact, rather than formed by the usual igneous processes.\nThis is especially evident in thin section 14310,564 which contains a breccia\nclast enclosed G, ~rystalline material.\n259\n\n,\n\n"\nt"\xc2\xb7]\n\nH\n\n:.\n\nI\n\nt,j\n\n?I\n\ni-)\n\nThe mesos tasis includ es late stage , very diver se mater ials.\nSome can be\nresolv ed into color less mater ial (~lass?) with opaque to\nreddis h brown\nspher ules, and high relie f fibers (apat ite?). It was\nplaced in documented bag 27N. Its lunar location and orientation are well\ndocumented.\nPHYSICAL CHARACTERISTICS\nMass\n\nDimensions\n\n144 g\n\n6 x f\\ x 4 cm\n\nThis is a medium light gray, coherent, fragmental breccia with a distinctive\nshape due to a prominent notch produced by spalling along two sets of fractures intersecting at an angle of 105\xc2\xb0.\nSURFACE FEATURES\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAll surfaces have a light to moderate density of glass-lined zap pits\n(20 - 30 per square centJmeter). The pits range from 0.1 - 1.0 mm in diameter (see Twedell et !l., 1978).\nSample 14313 is described by Morrison et al. (1972) as having large crater\njensities on all major surfaces, Its lunar orientation is well documented\nby lunar surface photography (Sutton et al., 1971). The tumbling history\n\\~as investigated and is presented in diagrammatic form in the article by\nMorrison et \xc2\xa31. (1972, p. 2776).\nThere are two well defined fractures, one of which extends along the length\nof the rock.\nPETROGRAPHIC DESCRIPTION\nThis sample is a polymict, fragmental breccia with clasts larger than 1 mm\ncomprising 25 - 30% of the rock, and fine-grained matrix the other 70 - 75%.\nThin section 14313,14 contains only about 10% lithic clasts (> 1 mm) with\nno mineral fragments that large. The clasts are angular to rounded rock fragments which appear to be mostly crystalline with well defined boundaries.\nThe clast composition ranges from 20% feldspar and 80% olivine in mesocratic\nclasts to 80% feldspar and 20% olivine in leucocratic clasts. Intermediate\nclasts contain 5 percent pyroxene. The matrix appears to be 70% clear subhedral feldspar grains 0.5 mm in size and 30% pyroxene grains < 0.1 mm in\nsize, with minor amounts of olivine of about 0.1 mm grain size. The matrix\nis texturally homogeneous but there are inhomogeneities in matrix mineralogy.\nThere is approximately 30% brownish-yellow "glass" in the matrix. There are\nabundant glass spheres and masses of glass present along with a small amount\nof spinel.\nDISCUSSION\nSample 14313 has been studied extensively by a consortium of nine labora270\n",\'.,\'"\n\nt\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\'\xc2\xb7\n0\xc2\xb7\n"\n\n..\n..\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n;\n\ntories. The mineralogy, petrology, and petrogenesis \\~ere investigated by\nFloran et al. (1972). Sample 14313 was described by them to be a coherent,\npolymic~breccia with a complex history of comminution and reagglomeration.\nFive thin sections were examined: 14313,7; 14313,39; 14313,40; 14313,41;\nand 14313,42, but these originated from only two parents: 14313,7 came\nfrom 14313,3 and the parent of the others is 14313,35. The dominant clast\ntypes were listed as (1) noritic rock fragments, (2) monomineralic fragments,\n(3) microbreccia clasts, and (4) glassy fragments including glass spherules.\nThese glass spherules are described as chondrules and chondrule-like bodies\nby King et al. (1972). Mare-type basalt clasts are described as rare by\nFloran e~a~ (1972). They found the matrix of the breccia to be composed\nprimarilY of fine particles of brownish glass. Varying degrees of shock\ndamage to the clasts were observed, ranging from unshocked through shockmelted fragments. A sequence of four unrer.rystallized microbreccias was\nrecognized and recrystallized cla~ts are also present. The abundance of\nmicronorite clasts in 14313 are interpreted as an indication that noritic\nrocks were an important pre-Imbrian rock type in the Imbrium Basin area.\n14313 was assigned to Group I of Lindsay\'s classification. He estimated 10%\nof the sample to be glass, most of which is colorless. Plagioclase and pyroxene are the dominant mineral phases. It was also described as a vitric matrix\nbreccia (VMB) by Simonds et \xc2\xa31. (1977), and as a regolith breccia by Quaide\nand Hrigley (1972). Chao et \xc2\xa31. (1972) list it as a compact, nonporous,\nregolith microbreccia (lb). (1972, 1974) measured the chemical compositions of lithic fragments, glasses, and chondrules in 14318, but did not ascertain their relative\nabundances. To obtain abundance data, the Imbrium Consortium (.QE.. cit.,\n1976) made a modal analysis of one thin section, took a census of all fragments larger than 1 mm in two sections, and compiled another census of\nparticles between 0.2 and 1.0 mm diam., in randomly select~d regions of\none section. The following discussion is a summary of thelr results:\nLithic fragments are of the same varieties as found in the Apollo 14 soils\n(e.g., Taylor et al., 1972) and other breccias (e.g., Chao etal., 1972).\nMost have KREEr-chemistry and are breccias, recrystallized to varying\ndegrees. Some are clearly polymict. Lithic fragments were classified as\nfollows: recrystallized noritic breccias, in which the matrix is finegrained and unmelted; partially melted noritic breccias, characterized\nby igneous-textured matri ces; granul iti c nod tic brecci as, with coarselycrystalline, equant matrices. Nonmare basalts, some of which could be\ntotal impact melts; mare-like basalts, which are distinguished from nonmare basalts by higher contents of mafic silicates and ilmenite; anorthositic (or ANT) rocks; and others, (ultramafic rocks, graphic intergrowths\nof quartz and alkal\'j-feldspar, and coarse-grained, unbrecciated noritic\nfragments). All but the last three categories are chemically equivalent\nto KREEP.\nGlasses are also of the same types as found in soils. They were classified\ninto the following groups: homogeneous glass, almost all of which were\npale yellow to bright yellow; ropy, or suevlte~like glass that contains\ncrystallites and mineral fragments; prown, swirly, agglutinitic-like glass;\nand devitrified glass. Chondrules were placed in a separate category, as\nwere mineral fragments.\nThe modal analysis, incorporating both clasts and matrix, was done on\nsection 14318,48, using a magnification of 80x. An area of about 6 cm 2\nwas scanned and 4,847 points were counted. Results are as follows:\nLithic fragments\nMineral fragments\nHomogeneous glass\nSuevite-like glass\nBrown, swirlY glass\nDevitri fied glass\nChondrules\n\n\'.\n\n~\n\n59.6%\n14.7%\n3.0%\n5.9%\n10.2%\n2.5%\n4.1%\n100.0%\n\nCompared to Apollo 14 breccias that are obvious soil breccias, such as\n14047 and 14055, rock 14318 is much richer in lithic fragments (60% vs.\n",lOj;) and poorer in brown, swirly matrix glass (10% vs. 50%). Nevertheless,\nthe same constituents normally found in lunar soils are present. Perhaps\n14318 was made from a relatively immature soil.\n\n287\n\n"\n\n,.\n\ni\n\nChondrules are confined to the < 1 mm fraction. These results are consistent\nwith those reported by Wilshire and Jackson (1972). Essentially the same\nproportions of types are found in each. Curiously, the. relative abundances\nof types of KREEPlithic fragments in 14318 is quite simila.r to those in\nthe freshest (least mature) soils from Apollo 14: 14142 from Cone Crater\nand 14151 from the bottom of a 30cm deep trench (Taylor et \xc2\xa31., 1972b);\nAs noted above,\xc2\xb7 it is possible that 14318 was formed from an immature soil.\n(The previous petrographic discussion of sample 14318 is from Interdisciplinary Studies by the Imbrium Consortium, volume 1, pp. 67-69.)\n\n;\n\nDISCUSSION\nSample 14318 is classified as an F2 by Wilshire and Jackson \'(1972) because\nof its coherence and abundant 1eucocrati c cl asts. They observe two distinct episodes of thermal metamorphism.\nThe first affected their 1A components producing well developed recrystallization textures lacked by their 1B lithic components, mineral clasts and\ndark matrix. Photographs of the models made of Apollo 14 samples are included\nin this section to acquaint the scientific community with their availability\nand to help lunar scientists identify the original location of their sample\nwithin the parent rock \xe2\x80\xa2\nGenealogies of several Apollo 14 samples have been made and are also included. These geneologies do not reflect any processing which has taken\nplace since the first thin section was made of each sample. It is hoped\nthat these methods of illustrating samples will prove useful. and will become a rOu",ne part of sample documentation procedure.\n\nJ\n\nl\n\nI\nI\nI\nI\n\n,\n\nJ\n,\n\nI j\n\nI\n\nI\n\n1\n\nI\n\n.~\nI .1\n\nI\n\nI,\nI\n\n,\n\nI\n\nI\n\ni\xc2\xb7\n\n1\n\nI\nI\n!\n\nj\n\nJ\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n1\n\ni\n\nI\n\nI\n\n1\n1\n\nI.\n\n~\n1\n\n),\n\nIi\n\n-1 j\n\n\'I\n\n1\n1\n\nI\xc2\xb7\xc2\xb7 \xe2\x80\xa2.\n\n[\n349\n\ni\ni\n,\n\n. 1\n\n...\n\n.\n.\n\n.;.-\n\n.\'1\'; 1\n\n\'.j\n\n!\n\n14301\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n,\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nE>\n,.-,\n\nLJ\n\no\no\n\n6:.\n\no\n\n1~\n\nEntirely subdivided\n\n,. ..,\n\nSowed\n\n1231\n0;; ;J\n\nChipped\nThin section\n\'25\'\n\nSawdust, sweepings\n\ne\n\n( ,\n\nhfJ\\\n\n\'. ~ h\nt~\n\n1291\n\n..\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n351\n\n\\~\n\n~\n\n>\n\ntl\n[I.\n\nf\n\n!-\n\nI\n\ni\n\nI\n\nr~f\n\nI\nI\'\nI\n\nI\n\ni\n\nI .\n, i\nI\nI\'\n\nI\n\nJ,.\n\nI I\nI.\n\n,\n\n\\J\n\nI\n\n[\n\n.1\n\n.1\n\nj\n\ni\nI\n\ni\n\nI\n\n!\n\n.J\n.\n\n,\n\n\\~\n\ni\n\n\'.\n\n.,\n\n-.1-------,1:,.-\n\nt\n\n..\n\n,..\n\'\n\n,\n\nj\n,\n\ni\nI\n,\n\ni !,\n\nI\n,.\n\n"\' ____ --1 _ .\n\nI\n\ni\n\n!\nt\n\n/4303\n\n!\nI\n\n\':~>\nl\n,\n\n\'.\n\n\'\n\n./\n\nI\'ii\n\ni\xc2\xb7\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI,\n\n,\n\nII\n11\n\n1\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI\nI\n\nI\n\n,\n\nr I\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI\n.. :i\n\nI\n\nI\nI\n\ni\nI\n\n(J)\n\n1\n.,.\n"I" \'.\n\nI\n\nn\n\n0\n0\n6\n\nSawed\nChipped\nThin section\nSawdust, sweepings\n\n0\n\nI\n,\ni\n\nI ..\n\n.\'\'"""L......;~\n\n"\n\n.
